	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">UK Independence Party</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=UKIP&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="UKIP">UKIP</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<p>The <b>UK Independence Party</b> (<b>UKIP</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/juː/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cute&#39;">juː</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Eurosceptic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing</a> to <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">political party in the <span class="nowrap">United Kingdom</span></a>. It currently has one representative in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, two Assembly Members (AMs) in the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a>, and one <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly" title="London Assembly">London Assembly</a> member. The party reached its greatest level of success in the mid-2010s, when it gained two <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament_(United_Kingdom)" title="Member of Parliament (United Kingdom)">Members of Parliament</a> and was the largest UK party in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>.
</p><p>UKIP originated as the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Federalist_League" title="Anti-Federalist League">Anti-Federalist League</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Single-issue_politics" title="Single-issue politics">single-issue</a> Eurosceptic party established in London by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Sked" title="Alan Sked">Alan Sked</a> in 1991. It was renamed UKIP in 1993 but its growth remained slow. It was largely eclipsed by the Eurosceptic <a href="/wiki/Referendum_Party" title="Referendum Party">Referendum Party</a> until the latter's 1997 dissolution. In 1997, Sked was ousted by a faction led by <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a>, who became the party's preeminent figure. In 2006, Farage officially became leader and under his direction the party adopted a wider policy platform and capitalised on concerns about rising immigration, in particular among the <a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">White British</a> working class. This resulted in significant breakthroughs at the <a href="/wiki/2013_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2013 United Kingdom local elections">2013 local elections</a>, <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2014 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2014 European elections</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 general election</a>. The pressure UKIP exerted on the government was the main reason for the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum" title="2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum">2016 referendum</a> which led to the UK's commitment to <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">withdraw from the European Union</a>. Farage then stepped down as UKIP leader, and the party's vote share and membership heavily declined. Following repeat leadership crises, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Batten" title="Gerard Batten">Gerard Batten</a> took over. Under Batten, UKIP moved into far-right territory by emphasising an anti-Islam message. At this, many longstanding members–including Farage–left and joined the new <a href="/wiki/Brexit_Party" title="Brexit Party">Brexit Party</a>.
</p><p>Ideologically positioned on the <a href="/wiki/Right-wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-wing">right-wing</a> of British politics, UKIP is characterised by <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political scientists</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_populist" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-wing populist">right-wing populist</a> party. UKIP's primary emphasis has been on Euroscepticism, calling for the United Kingdom's exit from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU). It promotes a <a href="/wiki/British_unionist" class="mw-redirect" title="British unionist">British unionist</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="British nationalist">British nationalist</a> agenda, encouraging a unitary British identity in opposition to growing <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">Welsh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_nationalism" title="Scottish nationalism">Scottish nationalisms</a>. Political scientists have argued that in doing so, it conflates <a href="/wiki/Britishness" title="Britishness">Britishness</a> with <a href="/wiki/Englishness" class="mw-redirect" title="Englishness">Englishness</a> and appeals to <a href="/wiki/English_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="English nationalist">English nationalist</a> sentiment. UKIP has also placed emphasis on lowering immigration, rejecting <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multiculturalism</a>, and opposing what it calls the "<a href="/wiki/Islamification" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamification">Islamification</a>" of Britain. Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>, it describes itself as economically <a href="/wiki/Libertarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian">libertarian</a> and promotes <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">liberal economic</a> policies. On social issues like <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="LGBT rights">LGBT rights</a>, education policy, and criminal justice it is <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">conservative</a>. Having an ideological heritage stemming from the right-wing of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>, it distinguishes itself from the mainstream political establishment through heavy use of <a href="/wiki/Populist" class="mw-redirect" title="Populist">populist</a> rhetoric, including describing its supporters as the "People's Army".
</p><p>Governed by its leader and National Executive Committee, UKIP is divided into twelve regional groups. A founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Direct_Democracy_in_Europe" title="Alliance for Direct Democracy in Europe">Alliance for Direct Democracy in Europe</a> <a href="/wiki/European_political_party" title="European political party">European political party</a>, most of UKIP's MEPs sit with the <a href="/wiki/Europe_of_Nations_and_Freedom" title="Europe of Nations and Freedom">Europe of Nations and Freedom</a> group in the European Parliament. While gaining electoral support from various sectors of British society, <a href="/wiki/Psephologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Psephologists">psephologists</a> established that at its height, UKIP's primary voting base consisted of older, working-class white men living in England. UKIP has faced a critical reception from mainstream political parties, much of the media, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-fascist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-fascist">anti-fascist</a> groups. Its discourse on immigration and cultural identity generated accusations of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, both of which it denies.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Foundation_and_early_years:_1991.E2.80.932004"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_and_early_years:_1991–2004">Foundation and early years: 1991–2004</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UKIP_Logo_(Original).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/UKIP_Logo_%28Original%29.svg/220px-UKIP_Logo_%28Original%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/UKIP_Logo_%28Original%29.svg/330px-UKIP_Logo_%28Original%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/UKIP_Logo_%28Original%29.svg/440px-UKIP_Logo_%28Original%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UKIP_Logo_(Original).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The initial UKIP logo.</div></div></div>
<p>UKIP began as the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Federalist_League" title="Anti-Federalist League">Anti-Federalist League</a>, a Eurosceptic political party established in 1991 by the historian <a href="/wiki/Alan_Sked" title="Alan Sked">Alan Sked</a>. The League opposed the recently signed <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a> and sought to sway the governing <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> toward <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">removing the United Kingdom</a> from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008256AbediLundberg200978FordGoodwin201421Tournier-Sol2015142_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008256AbediLundberg200978FordGoodwin201421Tournier-Sol2015142-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A former <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> candidate, member of the <a href="/wiki/Bruges_Group_(United_Kingdom)" title="Bruges Group (United Kingdom)">Bruges Group</a>, and professor at the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> (LSE), Sked had converted to Euroscepticism while teaching the LSE's European Studies programme.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201421_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201421-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Under the Anti-Federalist League's banner, Sked was a candidate for Member of Parliament (MP) for <a href="/wiki/Bath_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Bath (UK Parliament constituency)">Bath</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1992_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1992 United Kingdom general election">1992 general election</a>, gaining 0.2% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201422_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201422-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> At a League meeting held in the LSE on 3 September 1993, the group was renamed the UK Independence Party, deliberately avoiding the term "British" so as to avoid confusion with the far-right <a href="/wiki/British_National_Party" title="British National Party">British National Party</a> (BNP).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201422,_24_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201422,_24-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC3May13_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC3May13-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UKIP contested the <a href="/wiki/1994_European_Parliament_election" title="1994 European Parliament election">1994 European Parliament election</a> with little financing and much infighting, securing itself as the fifth largest party in that election with 1% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201424–25_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201424–25-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> During this period, UKIP was viewed as a typical <a href="/wiki/Single-issue_party" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-issue party">single-issue party</a> by commentators, some of whom drew comparisons with the French <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Poujade" title="Pierre Poujade">Poujadist movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20143_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20143-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Following the election, UKIP lost much support to the <a href="/wiki/Referendum_Party" title="Referendum Party">Referendum Party</a>; founded by the multi-millionaire <a href="/wiki/James_Goldsmith" title="James Goldsmith">James Goldsmith</a> in 1994, it shared UKIP's Eurosceptic approach but was far better funded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201426–27_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201426–27-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997 general election</a>, UKIP fielded 194 candidates and secured 0.3% of the national vote; only one of its candidates, <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a> in <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Salisbury (UK Parliament constituency)">Salisbury</a>, secured over 5% of the vote and had his deposit returned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201430_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201430-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> UKIP was beaten by the Referendum Party in 163 of the 165 seats in which they stood against each other.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201430_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201430-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Referendum Party disbanded following Goldsmith's death later that year and many of its candidates joined UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201432–33Etheridge201411_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201432–33Etheridge201411-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UKIP_bus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/UKIP_bus.jpg/220px-UKIP_bus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/UKIP_bus.jpg/330px-UKIP_bus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/UKIP_bus.jpg/440px-UKIP_bus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UKIP_bus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A UKIP <a href="/wiki/Campaign_bus" title="Campaign bus">campaign bus</a>, 2004</div></div></div>
<p>After the election, Sked was pressured into resigning by a party faction led by Farage, <a href="/wiki/David_Lott" title="David Lott">David Lott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Holmes_(politician)" title="Michael Holmes (politician)">Michael Holmes</a>, who deemed him too intellectual and dictatorial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201432–33_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201432–33-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Sked left the party, alleging that it had been infiltrated by <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racist</a> and far-right elements, including BNP spies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201436–37_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201436–37-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This connection was emphasised in the press, particularly when Farage was photographed meeting with BNP activists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201436–37_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201436–37-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Holmes took over as party leader, and in the <a href="/wiki/1999_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="1999 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">1999 European Parliament elections</a>—the first <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Elections in the United Kingdom">UK election</a> for the European Parliament to use <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>—UKIP received 6.5% of the vote and three seats, in <a href="/wiki/South_East_England_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="South East England (European Parliament constituency)">South East England</a> (Farage), <a href="/wiki/South_West_England_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="South West England (European Parliament constituency)">South West England</a> (Holmes), and the <a href="/wiki/East_of_England_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="East of England (European Parliament constituency)">East of England</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Titford" title="Jeffrey Titford">Jeffrey Titford</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201433–34_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201433–34-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An internal power struggle ensued between Holmes and the party's National Executive Committee (NEC), which was critical of Holmes after he called for the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> to have greater powers over the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>. Led by Farage, the NEC removed Holmes from power, and Titford was elected leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201435,_37Etheridge201411–12_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201435,_37Etheridge201411–12-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2001_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2001 United Kingdom general election">2001 general election</a>, UKIP secured 1.5% of the vote, and six of its 428 candidates retained their deposits. It had lost much of its support to the Conservatives, whose leader <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a> had adopted increasingly Eurosceptic rhetoric during his campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201438–41_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201438–41-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, the former Conservative MP <a href="/wiki/Roger_Knapman" title="Roger Knapman">Roger Knapman</a> was elected UKIP leader, bringing with him the experience of mainstream politics that the party had lacked.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201442_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201442-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Knapman hired the political campaign consultant <a href="/wiki/Dick_Morris" title="Dick Morris">Dick Morris</a> to advise UKIP. The party adopted the slogan "say no" and launched a national billboard campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201443–44_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201443–44-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, UKIP reorganised itself nationally as a <a href="/wiki/Private_company_limited_by_guarantee" title="Private company limited by guarantee">private company limited by guarantee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEtheridge201413–14_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEtheridge201413–14-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Growing_visibility:_2004.E2.80.932014"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Growing_visibility:_2004–2014">Growing visibility: 2004–2014</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG/170px-Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG/255px-Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG/340px-Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG 2x" data-file-width="398" data-file-height="515" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigel_Farage_Autumn_2008.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a>, leader of the party from 2006 to 2009 and again from 2010 to 2016, and <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEP</a> since 1999</div></div></div>
<p>UKIP's support increased during the <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2004 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2004 European Parliament elections</a>, when it placed third, securing 2.6&#160;million votes (16.1%) and winning twelve seats. This had been made possible through increased funding from major donors and the celebrity endorsement of chat show host <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kilroy-Silk" title="Robert Kilroy-Silk">Robert Kilroy-Silk</a>, who stood as a candidate in the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Midlands (European Parliament constituency)">East Midlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200980FordGoodwin20144,_44–46,_48_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200980FordGoodwin20144,_44–46,_48-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Kilroy-Silk then criticised Knapman's leadership, arguing that UKIP should stand against Conservative candidates, regardless of whether they were Eurosceptic or not. This position was rejected by many party members, who were uneasy regarding Kilroy-Silk. After Farage and Lott backed Knapman, Kilroy-Silk left the party in January 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201449–52_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201449–52-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Two weeks later, he founded his own rival, <a href="/wiki/Veritas_(political_party)" title="Veritas (political party)">Veritas</a>, taking a number of UKIP members—including both of its <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly" title="London Assembly">London Assembly</a> members—with him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200973FordGoodwin201466_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200973FordGoodwin201466-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Kilroy-Silk's defection, UKIP's membership declined by a third and donations dropped by over a half.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201462_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201462-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> UKIP continued to be widely seen as a single-issue party and in the <a href="/wiki/2005_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2005 United Kingdom general election">2005 general election</a>—when it fielded 496 candidates—it secured only 2.2% of the vote, and 40 candidates had their deposits returned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20145–6,_65_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20145–6,_65-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Electoral support for the BNP grew during this period, with academics and political commentators suggesting that the parties were largely competing for the same voter base, a section of about 20% of the UK population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDriver2011148FordGoodwin20147–8,_66–70_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDriver2011148FordGoodwin20147–8,_66–70-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Given that the BNP had outperformed UKIP in most of the seats that they both contested, many UKIP members, including several figures on the NEC, favoured an electoral pact with them, a proposal that Farage strongly condemned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201473–75_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201473–75-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, Farage was <a href="/w/index.php?title=2006_United_Kingdom_Independence_Party_leadership_election&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="2006 United Kingdom Independence Party leadership election (page does not exist)">elected leader</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008256FordGoodwin201471_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008256FordGoodwin201471-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> To attract support, he cultivated an image of himself as a "man of the people", openly smoking and drinking, showing disdain for the established parties, and speaking in an open manner that appeared unscripted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinlowHallTreadwell201741_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinlowHallTreadwell201741-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> He sought to broaden UKIP's image from that of a single-issue party by introducing an array of socially conservative policies, including reducing immigration, tax cuts, restoring <a href="/wiki/Grammar_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammar schools">grammar schools</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">climate change denial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012736FordGoodwin201472_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012736FordGoodwin201472-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In doing so he was attempting to attract disenfranchised former Conservatives who had left the party after its leader, <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, had moved in a <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">socially liberal</a> direction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201470–71GoodwinMilazzo201522_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201470–71GoodwinMilazzo201522-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> According to Farage, Cameron was "a <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a>" whose priorities were "gay marriage, foreign aid, and wind farms".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201563_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201563-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Cameron was highly critical of UKIP, referring to them as "fruitcakes, loonies, and closet racists".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDriver2011148FordGoodwin201471Etheridge201415GoodwinMilazzo20154–5_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDriver2011148FordGoodwin201471Etheridge201415GoodwinMilazzo20154–5-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives' largest donor, <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Wheeler" title="Stuart Wheeler">Stuart Wheeler</a>, donated £100,000 to UKIP after criticising Cameron's stance towards the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a> and the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> After trust in the mainstream parties was damaged by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_parliamentary_expenses_scandal" title="United Kingdom parliamentary expenses scandal">UK parliamentary expenses scandal</a>, UKIP received an immediate surge in support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201475–76_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201475–76-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> This helped it in the <a href="/wiki/2009_European_Parliament_election" title="2009 European Parliament election">2009 European Parliament election</a>, in which it secured 2.5&#160;million votes (16.5%), resulting in 13 MEPs, becoming the second largest party in the European Parliament after the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20146,_76_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20146,_76-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2009election_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009election-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> During the election, UKIP outperformed the BNP, whose electoral support base collapsed shortly after.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201477–78_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201477–78-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Malcolm_Pearson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Malcolm_Pearson.jpg/220px-Malcolm_Pearson.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Malcolm_Pearson.jpg/330px-Malcolm_Pearson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Malcolm_Pearson.jpg/440px-Malcolm_Pearson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="684" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Malcolm_Pearson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Malcolm Pearson led the party in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>In September 2009, Farage resigned as leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012737FordGoodwin201478_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012737FordGoodwin201478-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/2009_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="2009 UK Independence Party leadership election">leadership election</a> was won by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Pearson,_Baron_Pearson_of_Rannoch" title="Malcolm Pearson, Baron Pearson of Rannoch">Malcolm Pearson</a>, who emphasised UKIP's opposition to high immigration rates and <a href="/wiki/Islamism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Islamism in the United Kingdom">Islamism in Britain</a>, calling for a ban on the <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqa</a> being worn in public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012737FordGoodwin201480–83_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012737FordGoodwin201480–83-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012737FordGoodwin201482–84_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012737FordGoodwin201482–84-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Pearson was unpopular with the UKIP grassroots, who viewed him as an establishment figure too favourable to the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201481–83,_85_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201481–83,_85-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">2010 general election</a>, UKIP fielded 558 candidates and secured 3.1% of the vote (919,471 votes), but won no seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20148,_87_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20148,_87-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Pearson stood down as leader in August, and Farage was re-elected in the <a href="/wiki/2010_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="2010 UK Independence Party leadership election">leadership election</a> with more than 60% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201489_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201489-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201489Etheridge201416_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201489Etheridge201416-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Farage placed new emphasis on developing areas of local support through growth in local councils.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201494_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201494-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Observing that the party had done well in areas dominated by <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a> <a href="/wiki/Blue-collar_worker" title="Blue-collar worker">blue-collar workers</a> with no educational attainment, and that conversely it had done poorly in areas with high numbers of graduates and ethnic minorities, UKIP's campaign refocused directly at the former target vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201495_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201495-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> UKIP support would be bolstered by dissatisfaction with the <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">Conservative-Liberal Democrat coalition government</a> and the perception that its <a href="/wiki/Austerity" title="Austerity">austerity</a> policies benefited the socio-economic elite while imposing hardship on most Britons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016149–50_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016149–50-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> During this year, UKIP had witnessed far greater press coverage and growing support, with opinion polls placing it at around 10% support in late 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20149_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20149-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> UKIP put up a record number of candidates for the <a href="/wiki/2013_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2013 United Kingdom local elections">2013 local elections</a>, achieving its strongest local government result, polling an average of 23% in the wards where it stood, and increasing its number of elected councillors from 4 to 147.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015140_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015140-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-local_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-local-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This was the best result for a party outside the <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom#Major_parties_in_the_House_of_Commons" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">big three</a> in British politics since the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-GuardianMay3_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianMay3-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> with UKIP being described as "the most popular political insurgency" in Britain since the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a> during the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014225_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014225-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Entering_mainstream_politics:_2014.E2.80.932016"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Entering_mainstream_politics:_2014–2016">Entering mainstream politics: 2014–2016</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/European_Parliament_election%2C_2014_%28United_Kingdom%29.svg/170px-European_Parliament_election%2C_2014_%28United_Kingdom%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="317" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/European_Parliament_election%2C_2014_%28United_Kingdom%29.svg/255px-European_Parliament_election%2C_2014_%28United_Kingdom%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/European_Parliament_election%2C_2014_%28United_Kingdom%29.svg/340px-European_Parliament_election%2C_2014_%28United_Kingdom%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2142" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Results of the <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2014 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">European Parliament election, 2014</a> in Great Britain. Districts where UKIP received the largest number of votes are shown in purple.</div></div></div>
<p>In March 2014, <a href="/wiki/Ofcom" title="Ofcom">Ofcom</a> awarded UKIP "major party status".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016178_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016178-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2014_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2014 United Kingdom local elections">2014 local elections</a>, UKIP won 163 seats, an increase of 128, but did not take control of any council.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2014 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2014 European Parliament elections</a>, UKIP received the greatest number of votes (27.5%) of any British party, producing 24 MEPs.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201552_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201552-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The party won seats in every region of Britain, including its first in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-breakthrough_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breakthrough-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It made strong gains in traditionally <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> voting areas within Wales and the North of England; it for instance came either first or second in all 72 council areas of the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201564_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201564-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The victory established Farage and UKIP as "truly household names".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015104_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015104-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> It was the first time since 1906 that a party other than Labour or the Conservatives had won the most votes in a UK-wide election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20154_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20154-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UKIP gained its first MP when Conservative defector <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Carswell" title="Douglas Carswell">Douglas Carswell</a> won the seat of <a href="/wiki/Clacton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Clacton (UK Parliament constituency)">Clacton</a> during an <a href="/wiki/2014_Clacton_by-election" title="2014 Clacton by-election">October 2014 by-election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015124–129,_174_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015124–129,_174-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clacton_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clacton-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In November fellow Conservative defector <a href="/wiki/Mark_Reckless" title="Mark Reckless">Mark Reckless</a> became UKIP's second MP in a <a href="/wiki/2014_Rochester_and_Strood_by-election" title="2014 Rochester and Strood by-election">Rochester and Strood by-election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015135–136,_141–142,_174_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015135–136,_141–142,_174-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reckless_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reckless-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 general election</a>, UKIP secured over 3.8 million votes (12.6% of the total), replacing the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> as the third most popular party, but only secured one seat,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwin201513GoodwinMilazzo2015273DennisonGoodwin2015169ClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016137_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwin201513GoodwinMilazzo2015273DennisonGoodwin2015169ClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016137-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> with Carswell retaining his seat and Reckless losing his.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015269_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015269-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In the run-up to the election, Farage stated that he would resign as party leader if he did not win <a href="/wiki/South_Thanet_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South Thanet (UK Parliament constituency)">South Thanet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015257–258_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015257–258-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> On failing to do so, he resigned,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015289–291_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015289–291-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> although was reinstated three days later when the NEC rejected his resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015292–296_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015292–296-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> A period of 'civil war' broke out among senior membership between those who favoured Farage's leadership and those seeking a change.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015296_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015296-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>  In the <a href="/wiki/2015_Oldham_West_and_Royton_by-election" title="2015 Oldham West and Royton by-election">2015 Oldham West and Royton by-election</a> the party attacked <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a> as a security risk, but only gained a small increase in support at the expense of the Conservative Party.  In the 2016 <a href="/wiki/2016_National_Assembly_for_Wales_election" title="2016 National Assembly for Wales election">National Assembly for Wales election</a>, UKIP nearly tripled their share of votes (from 4.7 per cent to 12.5 per cent) and won seven seats.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To counter the loss of further votes to UKIP, the governing Conservatives promised a <a href="/wiki/2016_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum" title="2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum">referendum on the UK's continued membership of the EU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015126Reed2016228_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015126Reed2016228-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Rather than taking part in the official <a href="/wiki/Vote_Leave" title="Vote Leave">Vote Leave</a> campaign, to which various Eurosceptic Conservative and Labour politicians were linked, UKIP affiliated itself with the <a href="/wiki/Leave.EU" title="Leave.EU">Leave.EU</a> campaign group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a28_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a28-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Farage gained regular press coverage during the campaign, in which Leave.EU emphasised what it characterised as the negative impact of immigration on local communities and public services.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a28_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a28-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The June 2016 referendum produced a 51.89% majority in favour of leaving the EU: the accomplishment of UKIP's <i>raison d'être</i> raised questions about the party's future.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a27_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a27-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The loss of its MEPs would result in the loss of its primary institutional representation and a key source of its funding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a29_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2016a29-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Decline:_2016.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline:_2016–present">Decline: 2016–present</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/September_2016_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="September 2016 UK Independence Party leadership election">September 2016 UK Independence Party leadership election</a>, <a href="/wiki/November_2016_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="November 2016 UK Independence Party leadership election">November 2016 UK Independence Party leadership election</a>, <a href="/wiki/2017_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="2017 UK Independence Party leadership election">2017 UK Independence Party leadership election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2018_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="2018 UK Independence Party leadership election">2018 UK Independence Party leadership election</a></div>
<h4><span id="Farage.27s_resignation_as_leader.2C_Diane_James_and_Paul_Nuttall.27s_leadership"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Farage's_resignation_as_leader,_Diane_James_and_Paul_Nuttall's_leadership">Farage's resignation as leader, Diane James and Paul Nuttall's leadership</span></h4>
<p>After the referendum, Farage resigned as UKIP leader.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Diane_James" title="Diane James">Diane James</a> was elected as his successor, but resigned after 18 days and left the party in November 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Farage's former deputy, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nuttall" title="Paul Nuttall">Paul Nuttall</a>, was elected leader that month.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In March 2017, the party's only MP, Carswell, left the party to sit as an independent.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The next month, Reckless also left UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2017 United Kingdom local elections">2017 local elections</a>, UKIP lost all 145 seats it was defending, but gained one on <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_County_Council" title="Lancashire County Council">Lancashire County Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC050517_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC050517-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> These results led several prominent former UKIP members to call for the party to be disbanded.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In the following <a href="/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2017 United Kingdom general election">2017 general election</a>, UKIP received fewer than 600,000 votes and won no seats. The following day, Nuttall resigned and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Crowther" title="Steve Crowther">Steve Crowther</a> took over as interim party leader.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In July 2017, it lost its majority on <a href="/wiki/Thanet_District_Council" title="Thanet District Council">Thanet council</a> when Councillor Beverly Martin defected to the Conservatives;<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> in September all three UKIP councillors on <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_City_Council" title="Plymouth City Council">Plymouth council</a> defected to the Conservatives,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> as did <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Phillips_(Brexit_Party_politician)" title="Alexandra Phillips (Brexit Party politician)">Alexandra Phillips</a>, who had been UKIP's Head of Media for three years.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UKIP_logo_(2017).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/UKIP_logo_%282017%29.svg/150px-UKIP_logo_%282017%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="70" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/UKIP_logo_%282017%29.svg/225px-UKIP_logo_%282017%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fb/UKIP_logo_%282017%29.svg/300px-UKIP_logo_%282017%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="462" data-file-height="217" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UKIP_logo_(2017).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The UKIP logo, used briefly by the party from 2017 to 2018</div></div></div>
<h4><span id="Bolton.27s_leadership_and_Batten.27s_election_as_leader"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bolton's_leadership_and_Batten's_election_as_leader">Bolton's leadership and Batten's election as leader</span></h4>
<p>In 2017, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bolton_(British_politician)" title="Henry Bolton (British politician)">Henry Bolton</a>, a former soldier, was <a href="/wiki/2017_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="2017 UK Independence Party leadership election">elected</a> leader.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Two of the leadership candidates beaten by Bolton left the party: fourth place <a href="/wiki/John_Rees-Evans" title="John Rees-Evans">John Rees-Evans</a> announced plans to found a new political party, called Affinity, while second place <a href="/wiki/Anne_Marie_Waters" title="Anne Marie Waters">Anne-Marie Waters</a> set up a new political party, called <a href="/wiki/For_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="For Britain">For Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In January 2018, it was revealed that Bolton had left his wife for a relationship with the model Jo Marney; she was then suspended from UKIP for sending <a href="/wiki/Text_messaging" title="Text messaging">SMS messages</a> containing racist comments about <a href="/wiki/Prince_Harry" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Harry">Prince Harry</a>'s fiancée, <a href="/wiki/Meghan_Markle" class="mw-redirect" title="Meghan Markle">Meghan Markle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> That month, UKIP MEP <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Arnott" title="Jonathan Arnott">Jonathan Arnott</a> resigned from the party.<sup id="cite_ref-ukipresign_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ukipresign-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2018, UKIP's NEC delivered a <a href="/wiki/Motion_of_no_confidence" title="Motion of no confidence">vote of no confidence</a> in Bolton; only Bolton voted against the motion.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-vote_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-vote-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> He nevertheless refused to resign.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-vote_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-vote-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In protest, <a href="/wiki/Margot_Parker" title="Margot Parker">Margot Parker</a> resigned as deputy leader,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> as did the party's spokesmen for government, education, immigration, and trade and industry.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
A few days later, all seventeen UKIP members of <a href="/wiki/Thurrock_Council" title="Thurrock Council">Thurrock Council</a> left the party and formed <a href="/wiki/Thurrock_Independents" title="Thurrock Independents">Thurrock Independents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In February, UKIP members passed a vote of no confidence in Bolton, removing him as leader. He was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Batten" title="Gerard Batten">Gerard Batten</a> as interim leader until a new leadership election could be held.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> When the election occurred in April, Batten stood unopposed and was selected.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Batten.27s_leadership_and_association_with_the_far-right"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Batten's_leadership_and_association_with_the_far-right">Batten's leadership and association with the far-right</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="928" data-file-height="1254" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GerardBattenwithProtestors-cropped_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Under Batten's leadership, UKIP moved towards a far-right stance</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2018_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2018 United Kingdom local elections">2018 local elections</a>, UKIP lost 124 of the 126 seats it was defending, and gained a single seat in <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a> for a net loss of 123.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> MEP <a href="/wiki/James_Carver" title="James Carver">James Carver</a> left UKIP to sit as an independent on 28 May 2018, becoming the sixth UKIP MEP to leave since 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the leadership of Henry Bolton, party membership was understood to have slumped to around 18,000 by January 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> During Batten's interim leadership term, the party avoided insolvency after a financial appeal to members.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> As the new permanent leader, Batten focused the party more on opposing Islam and sought closer relations with the far-right activist <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Robinson_(activist)" title="Tommy Robinson (activist)">Tommy Robinson</a> and his followers.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The party saw its membership rise by 15% in July 2018, following the publication of the <a href="/wiki/Chequers_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Chequers Agreement">Chequers Agreement</a> and allowing three prominent far-right activists to join the party.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Previous leader <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a> stated he was "really upset" that Robinson could be allowed into the party and that he believed Gerard Batten was marginalising the party.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Batten's appointment of Robinson as an advisor was followed by a wave of high-profile resignations from the party. Farage announced his decision to resign in December 2018, calling Batten "obsessed" with Islam and saying that "UKIP wasn't founded to be a party based on fighting a religious crusade".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Former Deputy Chair <a href="/wiki/Suzanne_Evans" title="Suzanne Evans">Suzanne Evans</a> had left earlier that week after Batten survived a vote of confidence from the party NEC.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The former leader of the party in the Welsh Assembly, <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Jones_(politician)" title="Caroline Jones (politician)">Caroline Jones</a>, and the MEP <a href="/wiki/William_Legge,_10th_Earl_of_Dartmouth" title="William Legge, 10th Earl of Dartmouth">William Dartmouth</a> had also cited the party's trajectory to the right as reasons for leaving the party.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Another former leader, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nuttall" title="Paul Nuttall">Paul Nuttall</a>, also left for the same reason.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> By December 2018, a majority of the party's MEPs had left. Others leaving included <a href="/wiki/Peter_Whittle_(politician)" title="Peter Whittle (politician)">Peter Whittle</a>, the party's top vote-winner on the London Assembly.
</p><p>On 9 December 2018, before an important vote on Brexit legislation, UKIP led a "Brexit Betrayal" rally in central London fronted by Robinson, alongside prominent far-right groups.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> By April 2019, of 24 UKIP MEPs elected in the 2014 European Election, only 4 remained members of UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Ten of these MEPs had moved to Nigel's Farage's new party, <a href="/wiki/Brexit_Party" title="Brexit Party">The Brexit Party</a>, whilst most others choose to sit as <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">Independent MEPs</a>.
</p><p>By April 2019, it had become clear to the government of the United Kingdom that another extension to Brexit until 31 October 2019, which would therefore mean the United Kingdom would take part in the <a href="/wiki/2019_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2019 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2019 European Parliament elections</a>. UKIP announced it would take part in the elections, despite large losses to the newly formed Brexit Party. Candidates selected by UKIP to run in the election include right-wing <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> personalities, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin" title="Carl Benjamin">Carl Benjamin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Meechan" title="Mark Meechan">Mark Meechan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Benjamin has caused controversy by making "inappropriate" comments in 2016 about the rape threats of a female Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Jess_Phillips" title="Jess Phillips">Jess Phillips</a>, with the UKIP Swindon Branch chair calling for him to be deselected.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Videos made by Benjamin in which he uses racist terms also caused controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/2019_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2019 United Kingdom local elections">2019 United Kingdom local elections</a>, UKIP lost around 80% of the seats it was defending. The party was criticised for failing "to capitalise on the collapse of the Conservatives" by members of the media.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In the following 2019 European Parliament election, UKIP received 3.3% of the vote and lost all its remaining seats.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Braine.27s_election_and_leadership"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Braine's_election_and_leadership">Braine's election and leadership</span></h4>
<p>On 2 June 2019, Batten resigned his post as party leader as he had promised if he lost his MEP position.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Deputy leader <a href="/wiki/Mike_Hookem" title="Mike Hookem">Mike Hookem</a> resigned his position on 24 May 2019, intending to run for leadership, saying the party had lost its objective under Batten.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> On 1 August 2019, the party contested the <a href="/wiki/2019_Brecon_and_Radnorshire_by-election" title="2019 Brecon and Radnorshire by-election">Brecon and Radnorshire by-election</a>, coming in last-place behind the <a href="/wiki/Monster_Raving_Loony_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Monster Raving Loony Party">Monster Raving Loony Party</a>, with 0.8% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/2019_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="2019 UK Independence Party leadership election">2019 UKIP leadership election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Braine_(politician)" title="Richard Braine (politician)">Richard Braine</a> was elected UKIP leader and appointed Batten as deputy leader.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Braine was criticised for comments he has made which have been considered racist and offensive, including one incident in which he claimed he "often confused" London mayor <a href="/wiki/Sadiq_Khan" title="Sadiq Khan">Sadiq Khan</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Sidique_Khan" title="Mohammad Sidique Khan">Mohammad Sidique Khan</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/7_July_2005_London_bombings" title="7 July 2005 London bombings">7/7 terror attackers</a>. His tweet was condemned by Labour as "unacceptable racism".<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Braine later further came underfire when he announced he planned to boycott the September 2019 UKIP Party conference in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Wales" title="Newport, Wales">Newport</a>, after less than 450 tickets were sold for the conference. The Chairman of UKIP, Kirstan Herriot, stated to members that Braine had attempted to cancel the conference due to the low turnout and was highly critical of this attempted action.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology_and_policies">Ideology and policies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right-wing_populism">Right-wing populism</span></h3>
<p>UKIP is situated on the right wing of the <a href="/wiki/Left%E2%80%93right_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Left–right politics">left–right political spectrum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015147_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015147-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> More specifically, academic <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political scientists</a> and political commentators have described UKIP as a <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_populism" title="Right-wing populism">right-wing populist</a> party,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200972Jones2011245Dolezal2012142Liebert2012123Art2011188Driver2011149_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200972Jones2011245Dolezal2012142Liebert2012123Art2011188Driver2011149-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and as part of Europe's wider <a href="/wiki/Radical_right_(Europe)" title="Radical right (Europe)">radical right</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt2011188Driver2011149FordGoodwin201413BaleHoughVan_Kessel201397Goodwin201515Hayton2016402EvansMellon2016465_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt2011188Driver2011149FordGoodwin201413BaleHoughVan_Kessel201397Goodwin201515Hayton2016402EvansMellon2016465-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a>" refers to political groups which ideologically contrast "the people" against an elite or group of "dangerous others" whom the populists claim threaten the sovereignty of "the people",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016160_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016160-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and during its establishment in 1993, UKIP's founders explicitly described it as a <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a> party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015149_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015149-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> At the time, its "ideological heritage" lay within the right-wing of the Conservative Party,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and UKIP was influenced by the "<a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> populism" of Conservative politicians <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enoch_Powell" title="Enoch Powell">Enoch Powell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015147_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015147-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The party's growth is part of a wider rise in the prominence of right-wing populist groups across the Western world, and comparisons have been drawn between UKIP and the likes of the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a> in the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Finns_Party" title="Finns Party">True Finns</a> in Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed2016227–28ClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016136_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed2016227–28ClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016136-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Central to its populism is its defence of democracy and its claim to represent the true democratic will of the British people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015150_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015150-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg/220px-Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg/330px-Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg/440px-Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="684" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigel_Farage_of_UKIP.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farage at the 2009 UKIP Conference</div></div></div>
<p>The political scientists Amir Abedi and Thomas Carl Lundberg characterised UKIP as an "Anti-Political Establishment" party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200972,_74Dye20158_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200972,_74Dye20158-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The party's rhetoric presents the idea that there is a fundamental divide between the British population and the elite who govern the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200976Tournier-Sol2015149Dye20158_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200976Tournier-Sol2015149Dye20158-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> UKIP claims to stand up for ordinary people against this political elite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015150_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015150-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> UKIP politician <a href="/wiki/Bill_Etheridge" title="Bill Etheridge">Bill Etheridge</a> for instance claimed that his party represented "a democratic revolution... the people of Britain rising up and fighting to wrestle power from the elite".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEtheridge20148_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEtheridge20148-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Contributing to this anti-establishment message, Farage describes the party's supporters as "the People's Army",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201552Hayton2016406_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201552Hayton2016406-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and he regularly held photo-opportunities and journalistic interviews in a <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pub</a>, thus cultivating an "erudite everyman" image that contrasted with his past as a <a href="/wiki/Merchant_banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Merchant banking">merchant banker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016175_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016175-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UKIP uses recurring populist rhetoric—for instance by describing its policies as "<a href="/wiki/Common_sense" title="Common sense">common sense</a>" and "straight talking"—in order to present itself as a straightforward alternative to the mainstream parties and their supposedly elusive and complex discourse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015150_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETournier-Sol2015150-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> UKIP presents the UK's three primary parties—the Conservatives, Labour, and Liberal Democrats—as being essentially interchangeable, referring to them with the <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of "LibLabCon".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200976LynchWhitakerLoomes2012736Tournier-Sol2015150_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200976LynchWhitakerLoomes2012736Tournier-Sol2015150-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Farage accused all three parties of being <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social-democratic</a> in ideology and "virtually indistinguishable from one another on nearly all the key issues".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEtheridge201416_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEtheridge201416-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Farage has also accused the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> of being "the voice of anti-Englishness", suggesting that elements of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_nationalism" title="Scottish nationalism">Scottish nationalist</a> movement are "deeply racist, with a total hatred of the English".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016404_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016404-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalism_and_British_unionism">Nationalism and British unionism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>UKIP espouses a form of British nationalism; it states that it is a "civic" rather than an "ethnic" nationalism, although this categorisation has been disputed by political scientists<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>UKIP has always had the politics of <a href="/wiki/National_identity" title="National identity">national identity</a> at its core.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016400_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016400-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The party is nationalist, and its "basic claim—that the highest priority for the British polity is to assure that it is fully governed by the national state—is a nationalist one."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDye201511_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDye201511-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The party describes its position as being that of <a href="/wiki/Civic_nationalism" title="Civic nationalism">civic nationalism</a>, and in its manifesto explicitly rejects <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_nationalism" title="Ethnic nationalism">ethnic nationalism</a> by encouraging support from Britons of all ethnicities and religions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20147MycockHayton2014264_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin20147MycockHayton2014264-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Rejecting claims that it is racist, both Sked and later Farage described UKIP as a "non-racist, non-sectarian party".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201429Etheridge201415_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin201429Etheridge201415-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> In UKIP's literature, the party has placed an emphasis on "restoring Britishness" and counteracting what it sees as a "serious existential crisis" exhibited by the "<a href="/wiki/Islamification" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamification">Islamification</a>" of Britain, the "pseudo-nationalisms" of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and Ireland, and the multicultural and <a href="/wiki/Supranational_union" title="Supranational union">supranational</a> policies promoted by "the cultural left", describing its own stance as being "unashamedly unicultural".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that this attitude compromises the party's claim that its form of <a href="/wiki/British_nationalism" title="British nationalism">British nationalism</a> is civic and inclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UKIP considers itself to be a <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unionism in the United Kingdom">British unionist</a> party,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264Hayton2016401_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264Hayton2016401-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> although its support base is centred largely in England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Farage has characterised his party's growth as "a very English rebellion",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20158_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20158-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> and has described UKIP as "unashamedly patriotic, proud to be who we are as a nation".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20157_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20157-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The political scientist Richard Hayton argued that UKIP's British unionism reflects "Anglo-Britishness", a perspective that blurs the distinction between Britain and England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> With Mycock, Hayton argued that in conflating <a href="/wiki/English_national_identity" title="English national identity">Englishness</a> with <a href="/wiki/Britishness" title="Britishness">Britishness</a>, UKIP exhibited an "inherent Anglocentrism" that negates the distinct culture of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/People_of_Northern_Ireland" title="People of Northern Ireland">Northern Irish</a> peoples of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264_159-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014264-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Hayton suggests that UKIP tap into "a vein of nostalgic cultural nationalism" within England,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016402_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016402-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> and it has been noted that UKIP's discourse frames the image of Englishness in a nostalgic manner, harking back to the years before the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed2016228_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed2016228-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UKIP has emphasised the need to correct what it perceives as the United Kingdom's imbalance against England resulting from the "<a href="/wiki/West_Lothian_question" title="West Lothian question">West Lothian question</a>" and the <a href="/wiki/Barnett_formula" title="Barnett formula">Barnett formula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014265_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMycockHayton2014265-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The party has mobilised <a href="/wiki/English_nationalism" title="English nationalism">English nationalist</a> sentiment brought on by English concerns following the devolution within the UK and the rise of Welsh and Scottish nationalisms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed2016228Hayton2016405GoodwinMilazzo20155_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed2016228Hayton2016405GoodwinMilazzo20155-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The party initially opposed <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a> in the UK,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> criticising the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200975_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbediLundberg200975-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> However, in September 2011 Farage and the NEC announced their support for the establishment of an <a href="/wiki/English_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="English Parliament">English Parliament</a> to accompany the other devolved
