style="width:209px;max-width:209px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Reckless_AM_(27889492560).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Mark_Reckless_AM_%2827889492560%29.jpg/207px-Mark_Reckless_AM_%2827889492560%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="207" height="207" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Mark_Reckless_AM_%2827889492560%29.jpg/311px-Mark_Reckless_AM_%2827889492560%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Mark_Reckless_AM_%2827889492560%29.jpg/414px-Mark_Reckless_AM_%2827889492560%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Douglas Carswell and Mark Reckless, UKIP's only elected MPs. The former represented UKIP from 2014 till 2017; the latter from 2014 to 2015.</div></div></div></div>
<p>In the UK, the <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first-past-the-post voting</a> system for electing MPs to the House of Commons was a significant barrier to UKIP, whose support was widely distributed across different areas rather than being strongly focused in particular constituencies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014220–21_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014220–21-308">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> Further, the system encouraged <a href="/wiki/Tactical_voting" title="Tactical voting">tactical voting</a>, with many UKIP supporters believing that a vote for the party would be a <a href="/wiki/Wasted_vote" title="Wasted vote">wasted vote</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014221_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014221-309">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> Recognising this, Farage believed that the best way to win a seat in the House of Commons was to win a <a href="/wiki/By-election" title="By-election">by-election</a>, with UKIP contesting a number of these from 2010 onward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014241–42_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014241–42-310">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Over the next few years, it contested a number of by-elections around the country, coming second in both <a href="/wiki/2011_Barnsley_Central_by-election" title="2011 Barnsley Central by-election">Barnsley Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/2012_Rotherham_by-election" title="2012 Rotherham by-election">Rotherham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014243,_246GoodwinMilazzo201536_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014243,_246GoodwinMilazzo201536-311">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Spink" title="Bob Spink">Bob Spink</a>, the MP for <a href="/wiki/Castle_Point_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Castle Point (UK Parliament constituency)">Castle Point</a>, resigned the Tory <a href="/wiki/Whip_(politics)" title="Whip (politics)">whip</a> (becoming an <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">Independent</a>), but in April that year joined UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> However, in November he appeared again as an Independent in Commons proceedings,<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> ultimately losing the seat to a Conservative in <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">2010</a>.
</p><p>In 2014, two Conservative MPs changed allegiance to UKIP and resigned their seats to fight by-elections for UKIP. <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Carswell" title="Douglas Carswell">Douglas Carswell</a> won the <a href="/wiki/2014_Clacton_by-election" title="2014 Clacton by-election">Clacton by-election</a> on 9 October, making him the first MP to be elected representing UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Reckless" title="Mark Reckless">Mark Reckless</a> was also victorious in the Rochester and Strood by-election on 20 November.<sup id="cite_ref-reckless_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reckless-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
In the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 General Election</a>, Carswell kept his seat in Clacton but Reckless lost Rochester to the Conservative <a href="/wiki/Kelly_Tolhurst" title="Kelly Tolhurst">Kelly Tolhurst</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> UKIP had 3,881,129 votes (12.6%) and was the third largest party on vote share, yet it won only one seat.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> Because of this, there were calls from some in UKIP for a voting reform in favour of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> Carswell quit the party in March 2017 to become an independent, leaving UKIP without any MPs in the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
In the <a href="/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2017 United Kingdom general election">2017 election</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Snap_election" title="Snap election">snap election</a> initiated by PM <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> and scheduled for 8 June 2017, UKIP got 1.9% of the votes (after 12.6% in the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 election</a>) and no seats in the House of Commons.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Lords">House of Lords</span></h3>
<p>On 24 June 1995, UKIP gained its first member of the House of Lords, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Norton,_8th_Baron_Grantley" title="Richard Norton, 8th Baron Grantley">The Lord Grantley</a>, who had joined the party in 1993 from the Conservatives and had recently succeeded to his father's titles. However, with the coming <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Act_1999" title="House of Lords Act 1999">House of Lords Act 1999</a>, he decided not to stand for election as a continuing member, and so left the House in November 1999. Earlier in 1999, UKIP had gained a second peer in the House of Lords, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Thomas_Orlando_Bridgeman,_7th_Earl_of_Bradford" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Thomas Orlando Bridgeman, 7th Earl of Bradford">The Earl of Bradford</a>, but he, too, left the House in November 1999 because of the House of Lords Act. <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Pearson,_Baron_Pearson_of_Rannoch" title="Malcolm Pearson, Baron Pearson of Rannoch">The Lord Pearson of Rannoch</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Verney,_21st_Baron_Willoughby_de_Broke" title="David Verney, 21st Baron Willoughby de Broke">The Lord Willoughby de Broke</a> both defected to UKIP in 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012736_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012736-318">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> giving the party its first representation in the House of Lords since the departure of Lord Grantley and Lord Bradford.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> The Lord Pearson of Rannoch went on to serve as party leader from November 2009 to September 2010. On 18 September 2012, <a href="/wiki/David_Stevens,_Baron_Stevens_of_Ludgate" title="David Stevens, Baron Stevens of Ludgate">The Lord Stevens of Ludgate</a> joined UKIP, having sat as an Independent Conservative since his expulsion from the Conservatives in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> In Autumn 2018, Lord Willoughby de Broke left UKIP, reducing the party's representation in the upper house back down to two.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Lord Stevens also left the party, in December 2018, leaving former leader Lord Pearson as UKIP's sole peer.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_assemblies_and_parliaments">Regional assemblies and parliaments</span></h3>
<p>UKIP competes electorally in all four parts of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayton2016401-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
In October 2012, UKIP gained its first representation in a devolved Assembly the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> in <a href="/wiki/David_McNarry" title="David McNarry">David McNarry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Legislative_Assembly_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Member of the Legislative Assembly (Northern Ireland)">MLA</a> for <a href="/wiki/Strangford_(Assembly_constituency)" title="Strangford (Assembly constituency)">Strangford</a>, who had left the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> The party however failed to continue its representation at the <a href="/wiki/2016_Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election" title="2016 Northern Ireland Assembly election">2016 election</a>, coming within a hundred votes of taking a seat in <a href="/wiki/East_Antrim_(Assembly_constituency)" title="East Antrim (Assembly constituency)">East Antrim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-newsletter.co.uk_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsletter.co.uk-324">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UKIP's support has been particularly weak in Scotland, where it has no representatives in the devolved parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014244_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014244-325">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> UKIP fielded candidates at the <a href="/wiki/2011_Scottish_Parliament_election" title="2011 Scottish Parliament election">Scottish Parliament election on 5 May 2011</a>, when its platform included a commitment to keep the Scottish Parliament in Edinburgh, while replacing the separately-elected <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Scottish_Parliament" title="Member of the Scottish Parliament">Members of the Scottish Parliament</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Grand_Committee" title="Scottish Grand Committee">Members of the House of Commons elected in Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party also fielded candidates for the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2016_National_Assembly_for_Wales_election" title="2016 National Assembly for Wales election">2016 election</a>, it entered the Assembly for the first time, winning seven of 60 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> However, following the resignations of <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Jones_(politician)" title="Caroline Jones (politician)">Caroline Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Reckless" title="Mark Reckless">Mark Reckless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Gill" title="Nathan Gill">Nathan Gill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Brown" title="Michelle Brown">Michelle Brown</a>, by March 2019 the party's representation had fallen to three AMs.<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh3_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh3-329">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> UKIP ceased to have a formal Welsh Assembly group after <a href="/wiki/David_Rowlands_(Welsh_politician)" title="David Rowlands (Welsh politician)">David Rowlands</a> resigned in May 2019 to form a new Brexit Party group with Reckless, Jones and <a href="/wiki/Mandy_Jones_(politician)" title="Mandy Jones (politician)">Mandy Jones</a> (who had replaced Nathan Gill on his resignation as an AM).<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh2_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh2-330">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg/220px-UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg/330px-UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg/440px-UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_Independence_Party_offices_in_Tunbridge_Wells_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1731970.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>UKIP office in <a href="/wiki/Royal_Tunbridge_Wells" title="Royal Tunbridge Wells">Royal Tunbridge Wells</a></div></div></div>
<p>UKIP initially paid little attention to local government elections. However, this changed after Farage observed that building localised strongholds of support in various parts of the country had been the process by which the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> had entered the House of Commons, and that this was a strategy that could benefit UKIP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014250_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014250-331">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> UKIP subsequently focused on the <a href="/wiki/2011_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2011 United Kingdom local elections">2011 local elections</a>, in which it fielded over 1,100 candidates, winning seven and becoming the main opposition in over 100.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014250–51_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014250–51-332">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first UKIP local council election win occurred when one of its members was elected to <a href="/wiki/South_Cambridgeshire_District_Council" title="South Cambridgeshire District Council">South Cambridgeshire District Council</a> in 2000. A number of Conservative, Liberal Democrat, Labour and Independent local councillors in all four constituent nations of the UK defected to UKIP over subsequent years, with the most recent defections to date (May to July 2013) coming from former Conservative councillors in the London Boroughs of <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Merton" title="London Borough of Merton">Merton</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames" title="London Borough of Richmond upon Thames">Richmond upon Thames</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Havering" title="London Borough of Havering">Havering</a>, and from Labour in <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">Northampton</a> and <a href="/wiki/North-East_Lincolnshire" class="mw-redirect" title="North-East Lincolnshire">North-East Lincolnshire</a>. In May 2013, 33 English and one Welsh council held local elections, with UKIP gaining 139 seats for a total of 147, with significant gains in <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_County_Council" title="Lincolnshire County Council">Lincolnshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_County_Council" title="Norfolk County Council">Norfolk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kent_County_Council" title="Kent County Council">Kent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2013 local elections, UKIP won 147 seats and established itself as the largest opposition party in six English county councils.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014253_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014253-334">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/2013_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2013 United Kingdom local elections">2013</a> and <a href="/wiki/2014_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2014 United Kingdom local elections">2014</a> local elections, UKIP made significant gains to become the fourth largest party in terms of councillors in England, and fifth largest in the UK, with over 300 seats (out of about 21,000). In the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2015 United Kingdom local elections">2015 local elections</a>, UKIP took control of <a href="/wiki/Thanet_District_Council" title="Thanet District Council">Thanet District Council</a>, its first majority control of a council.<sup id="cite_ref-Council_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council-335">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> However, the party lost control later in the year after several of its councillors defected and it lost its majority. UKIP later took back control as a majority after winning the 2016 Northwood ward by-election, taking its number of councillors up to 29. In the 2016 local election, UKIP won 58 council seats, an increase of 25.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2017 United Kingdom local elections">2017 United Kingdom local elections</a>, UKIP lost all of the seats it was defending but gained one from Labour on <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_County_Council" title="Lancashire County Council">Lancashire County Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC050517_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC050517-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In the 
<a href="/wiki/2019_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2019 United Kingdom local elections">2019 United Kingdom local elections</a>, UKIP suffered severe losses, with its number of councillors collapsing by 145 to 31, in the districts where votes were held that year. Its worst result was in Thanet where it lost 33 councillors.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC050519_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC050519-337">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament">European Parliament</span></h3>
<p>As a result of its hard Eurosceptic approach, UKIP does not recognise the legitimacy of the European Parliament, and under Sked's leadership refused to take any of the EP seats that it won.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008257_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008257-338">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> This changed after 1997, when the party decided that its elected representatives would take such seats to publicise its anti-EU agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008257_338-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUsherwood2008257-338">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>
As a result of the <a href="/wiki/1999_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="1999 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">1999 European parliament election</a>, three UKIP MEPs were elected to the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>. Together with Eurosceptic parties from other nations, they formed a new <a href="/wiki/Political_groups_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Political groups of the European Parliament">European parliamentary group</a> called <a href="/wiki/Europe_of_Democracies_and_Diversities" title="Europe of Democracies and Diversities">Europe of Democracies and Diversities</a> (EDD).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhinnemoreMcGowan2013276_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhinnemoreMcGowan2013276-339">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg/220px-Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg/330px-Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg/440px-Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicolas_Dupont_Aignan_et_Nigel_Farage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farage with <a href="/wiki/France_Arise" class="mw-redirect" title="France Arise">France Arise</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Dupont-Aignan" title="Nicolas Dupont-Aignan">Nicolas Dupont-Aignan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, February 2013</div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2004 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2004 European parliament election</a>, 37 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a> from the UK, <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Parliament_election_in_Poland" title="2004 European Parliament election in Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Parliament_election_in_Denmark" title="2004 European Parliament election in Denmark">Denmark</a> and <a href="/wiki/2004_European_Parliament_election_in_Sweden" title="2004 European Parliament election in Sweden">Sweden</a> founded a new European Parliamentary group called <a href="/wiki/Independence_and_Democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Independence and Democracy">Independence and Democracy</a> as a direct successor to the EDD group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhinnemoreMcGowan2013276Thorlakson201372_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhinnemoreMcGowan2013276Thorlakson201372-340">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/2009_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2009 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2009 European parliament election</a>, UKIP was a founder member of a new right-wing grouping called <a href="/wiki/Europe_of_Freedom_and_Democracy" title="Europe of Freedom and Democracy">Europe of Freedom and Democracy</a> (EFD) comprising Eurosceptic, radical right, nationalist, national-conservative and other political factions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012739Considère-Charon2010158Staab201167_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynchWhitakerLoomes2012739Considère-Charon2010158Staab201167-341">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> This group was more right-wing than the previous term's Independence and Democracy group.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2014 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">2014 European parliament election</a>, the EFD group was reconstituted as the <a href="/wiki/Europe_of_Freedom_and_Direct_Democracy" title="Europe of Freedom and Direct Democracy">Europe of Freedom and Direct Democracy</a> (EFDD or EFD<sup>2</sup>) group on 24 June 2014, with a significant changes to group composition, including the <a href="/wiki/Five_Star_Movement" title="Five Star Movement">Five Star Movement</a> of Italy, a total of 48 members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015116–118_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015116–118-343">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> The EFDD group lost official status in October 2014 when the defection of the Latvian MEP <a href="/wiki/Iveta_Grigule" class="mw-redirect" title="Iveta Grigule">Iveta Grigule</a> meant its membership no longer met the required number of states for Parliamentary groups (at least seven different member states).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015118_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015118-345">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> On 20 October, the EFDD announced it had restored the requisite seven state diversity by recruiting <a href="/wiki/Robert_Iwaszkiewicz" title="Robert Iwaszkiewicz">Robert Iwaszkiewicz</a>, one of four representatives of the far-right Polish party <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_New_Right" title="Congress of the New Right">Congress of the New Right</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015118_345-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015118-345">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> In December 2014 UKIP co-founded the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Direct_Democracy_in_Europe" title="Alliance for Direct Democracy in Europe">Alliance for Direct Democracy in Europe</a>, a <a href="/wiki/European_political_party" title="European political party">European political party</a> whose membership is composed of several member parties of the EFDD parliamentary group.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2009–14 parliament, UKIP ranked 76th out of 76 for attendance, took part in 61% of votes, and had three of the six lowest attending MEPs,<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> which led to criticism from other parties and ex-UKIP MEPs that low participation may damage British interests.<sup id="cite_ref-times14_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times14-350">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> Between July 2014 and May 2015, its 23 MEPs maintained their record as the least active, participating on average in only 62.29% of votes.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> In response to criticism of low participation by UKIP MEPs in the EU Parliament, Farage has said that "Our objective as MEPs is not to keep voting endlessly for more EU legislation and to take power away from Westminster."<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Members_of_the_European_Parliament">Members of the European Parliament</span></h4>
<p>UKIP has no members in the European Parliament following the 2019 EU election. Twenty-four UKIP representatives were elected in the 2014 election, but twenty have since defected, one was expelled<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> and three lost their seats in the 2019 election.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For a full list of defections see <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_politicians_who_have_crossed_the_floor#List_of_Members_of_the_European_Parliament_who_have_crossed_the_floor" class="mw-redirect" title="List of British politicians who have crossed the floor">List of British politicians who have crossed the floor#List of Members of the European Parliament who have crossed the floor</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_Carver" title="James Carver">James Carver</a> left UKIP to sit as an independent on 28 May 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Legge,_10th_Earl_of_Dartmouth" title="William Legge, 10th Earl of Dartmouth">William Dartmouth</a> left the party on 26 September 2018 to sit as an independent, accusing Batten of "hijacking the party to campaign against Islam as a religion" and associating himself with "outlandish people and extreme right-wing groups".<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Etheridge" title="Bill Etheridge">Bill Etheridge</a> followed shortly afterwards, on 2 October 2018, saying that the party under Batten's leadership "is seen by voters as a vehicle of hate towards Muslims and the gay community".<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2018, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_O%27Flynn" title="Patrick O&#39;Flynn">Patrick O'Flynn</a> resigned to join the 'rump' <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK,_1990%E2%80%93present)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK, 1990–present)">Social Democratic Party</a> in protest over the party's move to the "hard right",<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Louise_Bours" title="Louise Bours">Louise Bours</a> is now independent.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> Former leader Nigel Farage quit on 6 December 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> as did Scottish MEP David Coburn.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> Another former leader <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nuttall" title="Paul Nuttall">Paul Nuttall</a> quit the party the following day as did <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly" title="London Assembly">London Assembly</a> Member <a href="/wiki/Peter_Whittle_(politician)" title="Peter Whittle (politician)">Peter Whittle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> It was reported that <a href="/wiki/Tim_Aker" title="Tim Aker">Tim Aker</a> had also quietly quit the party earlier in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julia_Reid" title="Julia Reid">Julia Reid</a> announced her resignation from UKIP on 8 December 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Bullock" title="Jonathan Bullock">Jonathan Bullock</a> following the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jill_Seymour" title="Jill Seymour">Jill Seymour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Collins" title="Jane Collins">Jane Collins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margot_Parker" title="Margot Parker">Margot Parker</a> left for the Brexit Party on 15 April 2019, with the first of those three citing the party's current direction and occupation of 'the extreme right of politics'<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> and the second citing Batten's 'sick' defence of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin#Rape_comments" title="Carl Benjamin">Carl Benjamin's rape comments</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> On 17 April, Jonathan Arnott and Ray Finch both defected to The Brexit Party and along with Seymour, Collins and Parker sat in the EFDD group.<sup id="cite_ref-europarlEFDD_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarlEFDD-368">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of April 2019, Batten and Agnew were members of the <a href="/wiki/Europe_of_Nations_and_Freedom" title="Europe of Nations and Freedom">Europe of Nations and Freedom</a> group in the European Parliament<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> while Hookem was <i><a href="/wiki/Non-Inscrit" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-Inscrit">Non-Inscrit</a></i> (unattached).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_results">Election results</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party_representation_and_election_results" title="UK Independence Party representation and election results">UK Independence Party representation and election results</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Elections">General Elections</span></h3>
<p>During the 2010–15 Parliament, two Conservative MPs defected to UKIP and were re-elected in subsequent by-elections. At the 2015 general election, UKIP retained one of these seats (<a href="/wiki/Clacton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Clacton (UK Parliament constituency)">Clacton</a>) and received over 30% of the vote in <a href="/wiki/Boston_and_Skegness_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Boston and Skegness (UK Parliament constituency)">Boston and Skegness</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Thanet_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South Thanet (UK Parliament constituency)">South Thanet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heywood_and_Middleton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Heywood and Middleton (UK Parliament constituency)">Heywood and Middleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thurrock_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Thurrock (UK Parliament constituency)">Thurrock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rochester_and_Strood_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Rochester and Strood (UK Parliament constituency)">Rochester and Strood</a>.  They lost their only seat in the 2017 election, as Clacton was regained by the Conservatives.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_political_groups">Other political groups</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg/220px-Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg/330px-Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg/440px-Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2351" data-file-height="1609" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Corbyn_security_risk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A UKIP posted accusing Labour Party leader <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a> of being a security risk; put up during the 2015 Oldham West and Royton by-election</div></div></div>
<p>In campaigning on emotive issues, UKIP has proved divisive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015xiii_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015xiii-376">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> Popular stereotypes have framed it as a far-right party,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201592_288-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201592-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> and portrayed its activists as old white men holding offensive views.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015111_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015111-377">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> The party has faced vocal opposition from <a href="/wiki/Anti-fascism" title="Anti-fascism">anti-fascist</a> groups such as <a href="/wiki/Hope_not_Hate" title="Hope not Hate">Hope not Hate</a>, who have accused it of tapping into nationalist and xenophobic sentiment in its campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014147_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014147-378">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup> Writing for <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times_Magazine" title="The New York Times Magazine">The New York Times Magazine</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Wheatcroft" title="Geoffrey Wheatcroft">Geoffrey Wheatcroft</a> noted that there had been "a concerted campaign to brand UKIP as racist, an accusation that some of its own activists have done nothing to discourage."<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> Goodwin and Caitlin Milazzo highlighted that Farage had been "routinely ridiculed and dismissed", at best being portrayed as "a beer-swilling populist who wanted to drag Britain back to the 1950s" while at worst depicted as "a racist... would-be demagogue" who secretly wanted to overthrow the UK's liberal <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20153_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo20153-380">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For many years, mainstream political figures derided or demeaned the importance of UKIP, although this did little to obstruct its electoral advances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016172_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016172-381">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> By 2014, at which point UKIP was securing significant electoral support in the European Parliamentary elections, the main parties began to take it more seriously and devoted more time to countering the electoral threat it posed to them, in turn drawing more journalistic attention to the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016177_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016177-382">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> This increased attention gave the party the "oxygen of publicity" which helped bring the party to the attention of previously inattentive voters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016144_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkeWhiteleyBorgesSanders2016144-383">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> Many on Britain's centre-left have been reluctant to accept that UKIP was hindering public support for Labour, instead believing that they were primarily a problem for the Conservatives and would thus help produce a Labour victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwin201515_384-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwin201515-384">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201562_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201562-385">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup> Labour found that their campaign strategy of accusing UKIP of racism backfired, as rather than distancing UKIP supporters from the party it contributed to the perception that Labour failed to understand widespread concerns regarding immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201543–44_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201543–44-386">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> A December 2014 poll by <a href="/wiki/ComRes" title="ComRes">ComRes</a> found that voters saw UKIP as closer to the centre-ground of politics than the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_academia">Media and academia</span></h3>
<p>The British press have publicised statements made by UKIP activists and candidates which have been regarded as racist, sexist or otherwise bigoted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014282–83GoodwinMilazzo201546–47_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFordGoodwin2014282–83GoodwinMilazzo201546–47-388">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> Among the examples of UKIP representatives and supporters embarrassing the party have been an MEP who called for a ban on the construction of <a href="/wiki/Mosques" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosques">mosques</a> and for all British Muslims to sign a code of conduct, a councillor who suggested that shops should be allowed to refuse service to women and homosexuals, and a council candidate who compared Islam to <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> and told black comedian <a href="/wiki/Lenny_Henry" title="Lenny Henry">Lenny Henry</a> to leave Britain after the latter called for greater ethnic diversity within the UK's creative industries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201548_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201548-389">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, a documentary called <i>Meet the Ukippers</i> filmed activists making racist statements; one said "the only people I do have a problem with are negroes".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015231_189-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo2015231-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> For many years such individuals were internally tolerated within the party, although as part of Farage's push to professionalise the party a number of its members, such as MEP <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Bloom" title="Godfrey Bloom">Godfrey Bloom</a>, were expelled for making comments that brought UKIP into disrepute.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016173–74_390-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016173–74-390">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup> In 2018, Jo Marney—who was then the girlfriend of the party leader Henry Bolton—was suspended from UKIP after it was revealed that she had sent texts stating that black Africans were "ugly". In these messages, she had criticised <a href="/wiki/Meghan_Markle" class="mw-redirect" title="Meghan Markle">Meghan Markle</a> for marrying into the British royal family, stating that Markle was "a dumb little commoner" and "a black American. Pushing their way to the top slowly. Next will be a Muslim PM and a black king."<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg/220px-Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg/330px-Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg/440px-Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4752" data-file-height="3168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nigel_Farage_talking_to_the_media.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farage talking to the media in 2012</div></div></div>
<p>In a May 2014 <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> survey, 47% considered the media to be biased against UKIP, which was double the percentage who deemed the media biased against any other party.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201548_389-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwinMilazzo201548-389">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> received almost 1,200 complaints about its coverage of the 2014 European and local elections; 149 claimed that the BBC were biased against UKIP, while the rest claimed that it gave disproportionate attention to the party. The BBC defended its coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup> Farage accused the BBC of a "liberal bias", particularly on issues of immigration, the EU, and climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>David Deacon and Dominic Wring's examination of press coverage of UKIP during their 2014 campaign demonstrated that of the elite newspapers, the pro-EU titles <i>The Guardian</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> gave the most coverage to perceived racist and intolerant aspects of the party, while the Eurosceptic titles <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i> instead focused on questioning the propriety and integrity of UKIP representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016180_395-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016180-395">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> Among the populist tabloids, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">The Sun</a></i>/<i><a href="/wiki/Sun_on_Sunday" class="mw-redirect" title="Sun on Sunday">Sun on Sunday</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i>/<i><a href="/wiki/Sunday_Mirror" title="Sunday Mirror">Sunday Mirror</a></i> were found to contain the most negative coverage of UKIP, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sunday_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunday Express">Sunday Express</a></i>—owned by UKIP donor Richard Desmond—gave significantly lower coverage to the gaffes and prejudices of UKIP representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016180_395-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016180-395">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> Deacon and Wring noted that the majority of those right-wing newspapers that share UKIP's views on immigration also share the perspective of more liberal newspapers that many of UKIP's interventions are racist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016182_396-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016182-396">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup> This right-wing press opposition to UKIP may result from the allegiance that these newspapers have to the Conservatives, and resulting perception of UKIP as an electoral threat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016182_396-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeaconWring2016182-396">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Academic research has been carried out into UKIP. In 2016, it was noted that most of this had focused on examining the party's electoral support base, its consequences for other parties, and the possibilities and prospects of a referendum on continued EU membership, with little having focused on an examination of the party's policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReed2016226_397-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReed2016226-397">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup> Two currents have emerged among those seeking to interpret UKIP: the first, and generally older, current views them as a manifestation of Britain's strong Eurosceptic movement, while the second seeks to explain their position in the UK parliamentary system while drawing upon the comparative literature on right-wing populist parties elsewhere in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDye20153–4_398-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDye20153–4-398">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-legalleader-301"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-legalleader_301-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Diane James won the <a href="/wiki/September_2016_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="September 2016 UK Independence Party leadership election">September 2016 leadership election</a> but resigned 18 days later, prior to taking office. As the relevant paperwork required by the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_Kingdom)" title="Electoral Commission (United Kingdom)">Electoral Commission</a> was not completed before her resignation, legally Farage remained the leader of UKIP during James's tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> Farage continued to act as interim leader of UKIP until the <a href="/wiki/November_2016_UK_Independence_Party_leadership_election" title="November 2016 UK Independence Party leadership election">November 2016</a> election.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
